<meta name="DC.Title" content="Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1126/science.1253435" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2014-08-22" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Association for the Advancement of Science" />

<meta name="DC.Relation" content="10.1126/science.1260782" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Many domesticated plants arose through the meeting of multiple genomes through hybridization and genome doubling, known as polyploidy. Chalhoub et al. sequenced the polyploid genome of Brassica napus , which originated from a recent combination of two distinct genomes approximately 7500 years ago and gave rise to the crops of rape oilseed (canola), kale, and rutabaga. B. napus has undergone multiple events affecting differently sized genetic regions where a gene from one progenitor species has been converted to the copy from a second progenitor species. Some of these gene conversion events appear to have been selected by humans as part of the process of domestication and crop improvement.

Science , this issue p. [950][1]

Oilseed rape ( Brassica napus L.) was formed ~7500 years ago by hybridization between B. rapa and B. oleracea , followed by chromosome doubling, a process known as allopolyploidy. Together with more ancient polyploidizations, this conferred an aggregate 72× genome multiplication since the origin of angiosperms and high gene content. We examined the B. napus genome and the consequences of its recent duplication. The constituent An and Cn subgenomes are engaged in subtle structural, functional, and epigenetic cross-talk, with abundant homeologous exchanges. Incipient gene loss and expression divergence have begun. Selection in B. napus oilseed types has accelerated the loss of glucosinolate genes, while preserving expansion of oil biosynthesis genes. These processes provide insights into allopolyploid evolution and its relationship with crop domestication and improvement.

 [1]: /lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1253435" />

&lt;p&gt;Many domesticated plants arose through the meeting of multiple genomes through hybridization and genome doubling, known as polyploidy. Chalhoub &lt;i&gt;et al.&lt;/i&gt; sequenced the polyploid genome of &lt;i&gt;Brassica napus&lt;/i&gt;, which originated from a recent combination of two distinct genomes approximately 7500 years ago and gave rise to the crops of rape oilseed (canola), kale, and rutabaga. &lt;i&gt;B. napus&lt;/i&gt; has undergone multiple events affecting differently sized genetic regions where a gene from one progenitor species has been converted to the copy from a second progenitor species. Some of these gene conversion events appear to have been selected by humans as part of the process of domestication and crop improvement.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Science&lt;/i&gt;, this issue p. 950&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/345/6199/950/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Many domesticated plants arose through the meeting of multiple genomes through hybridization and genome doubling, known as polyploidy. Chalhoub et al. sequenced the polyploid genome of Brassica napus , which originated from a recent combination of two distinct genomes approximately 7500 years ago and gave rise to the crops of rape oilseed (canola), kale, and rutabaga. B. napus has undergone multiple events affecting differently sized genetic regions where a gene from one progenitor species has been converted to the copy from a second progenitor species. Some of these gene conversion events appear to have been selected by humans as part of the process of domestication and crop improvement.

Science , this issue p. [950][1]

Oilseed rape ( Brassica napus L.) was formed ~7500 years ago by hybridization between B. rapa and B. oleracea , followed by chromosome doubling, a process known as allopolyploidy. Together with more ancient polyploidizations, this conferred an aggregate 72× genome multiplication since the origin of angiosperms and high gene content. We examined the B. napus genome and the consequences of its recent duplication. The constituent An and Cn subgenomes are engaged in subtle structural, functional, and epigenetic cross-talk, with abundant homeologous exchanges. Incipient gene loss and expression divergence have begun. Selection in B. napus oilseed types has accelerated the loss of glucosinolate genes, while preserving expansion of oil biosynthesis genes. These processes provide insights into allopolyploid evolution and its relationship with crop domestication and improvement.

 [1]: /lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1253435" />

(function( w ){
  // rel=preload support test
  if( !w.loadCSS ){
    return;
  }
  var rp = loadCSS.relpreload = {};
  rp.support = function(){
    try {
      return w.document.createElement( "link" ).relList.supports( "preload" );
    } catch (e) {
      return false;
    }
  };

  // loop preload links and fetch using loadCSS
  rp.poly = function(){
    var links = w.document.getElementsByTagName( "link" );
    for( var i = 0; i < links.length; i++ ){
      var link = links[ i ];
      if( link.rel === "preload" && link.getAttribute( "as" ) === "style" ){
        w.loadCSS( link.href, link );
        link.rel = null;
      }
    }
  };

  // if link[rel=preload] is not supported, we must fetch the CSS manually using loadCSS
  if( !rp.support() ){
    rp.poly();
    var run = w.setInterval( rp.poly, 300 );
    if( w.addEventListener ){
      w.addEventListener( "load", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } );
    }

        w.clearInterval( run );
      } )
    }

    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_sci_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_sci_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-corrections">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="alert alert--error messages highwire-corrections highwire-correction-type-correction"><h3 class="highwire-corrections-title">This article has a correction. Please see:</h3><ul class="highwire-corrections-list"><li class="relation-392837 first last"><a href="/content/345/6202/1260782" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Is corrected by - September 12, 2014</a></li>
</ul></div>  </div>

  
  </div>
<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" data-highwire-glossary-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="section editor-summary" id="abstract-1"><h2>The genomic origins of rape oilseed</h2><p id="p-2">Many domesticated plants arose through the meeting of multiple genomes through hybridization and genome doubling, known as polyploidy. Chalhoub <em>et al.</em> sequenced the polyploid genome of <em>Brassica napus</em>, which originated from a recent combination of two distinct genomes approximately 7500 years ago and gave rise to the crops of rape oilseed (canola), kale, and rutabaga. <em>B. napus</em> has undergone multiple events affecting differently sized genetic regions where a gene from one progenitor species has been converted to the copy from a second progenitor species. Some of these gene conversion events appear to have been selected by humans as part of the process of domestication and crop improvement.</p><p id="p-3"><em>Science</em>, this issue p. <a href="/lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1253435">950</a></p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-3"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="p-5">Oilseed rape (<em>Brassica napus</em> L.) was formed ~7500 years ago by hybridization between <em>B. rapa</em> and <em>B. oleracea</em>, followed by chromosome doubling, a process known as allopolyploidy. Together with more ancient polyploidizations, this conferred an aggregate 72× genome multiplication since the origin of angiosperms and high gene content. We examined the <em>B. napus</em> genome and the consequences of its recent duplication. The constituent A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes are engaged in subtle structural, functional, and epigenetic cross-talk, with abundant homeologous exchanges. Incipient gene loss and expression divergence have begun. Selection in <em>B. napus</em> oilseed types has accelerated the loss of glucosinolate genes, while preserving expansion of oil biosynthesis genes. These processes provide insights into allopolyploid evolution and its relationship with crop domestication and improvement.</p>
</div><p id="p-6">The Brassicaceae are a large eudicot family (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>) and include the model plant <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>. Brassicas have a propensity for genome duplications (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>) and genome mergers (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>). They are major contributors to the human diet and were among the earliest cultigens (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3"><em>3</em></a>).</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F1">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Recurrent genome duplications in B. napus.Genomic alignments between the basal angiosperm Amborella trichopoda (24), the basal eudicot Vitis vinifera (25), and the model crucifer A. thaliana, as well as B. rapa (9), B. oleracea (10, 11), and B. napus, are shown. A typical ancestral region in Amborella is expected to match up to 72 regions in B. napus (69 were detected for this specific region). Gray wedges in the background highlight conserved synteny blocks with more than 10 gene pairs." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-455280786" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 1&lt;/span&gt;  &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;Recurrent genome duplications in &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-7&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Genomic alignments between the basal angiosperm &lt;em&gt;Amborella trichopoda&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-24-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-24&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;24&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;), the basal eudicot &lt;em&gt;Vitis vinifera&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-25-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-25&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;25&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;), and the model crucifer &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt;, as well as &lt;em&gt;B. rapa&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-9-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-9&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;9&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;), &lt;em&gt;B. oleracea&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-10-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-10&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;10&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-11-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-11&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;11&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;), and &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt;, are shown. A typical ancestral region in &lt;em&gt;Amborella&lt;/em&gt; is expected to match up to 72 regions in &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt; (69 were detected for this specific region). Gray wedges in the background highlight conserved synteny blocks with more than 10 gene pairs.&lt;/p&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F1-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F1.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F1-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F1.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 1"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/573631" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F1-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 1</span> 
<span class="caption-title">Recurrent genome duplications in <em>B. napus</em>.</span>
<p id="p-7" class="first-child">Genomic alignments between the basal angiosperm <em>Amborella trichopoda</em> (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24"><em>24</em></a>), the basal eudicot <em>Vitis vinifera</em> (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25"><em>25</em></a>), and the model crucifer <em>A. thaliana</em>, as well as <em>B. rapa</em> (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>), <em>B. oleracea</em> (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10"><em>10</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>), and <em>B. napus</em>, are shown. A typical ancestral region in <em>Amborella</em> is expected to match up to 72 regions in <em>B. napus</em> (69 were detected for this specific region). Gray wedges in the background highlight conserved synteny blocks with more than 10 gene pairs.</p>
<div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-8"><em>B. napus</em> (genome A<sub>n</sub>A<sub>n</sub>C<sub>n</sub>C<sub>n</sub>) was formed by recent allopolyploidy between ancestors of <em>B. oleracea</em> (Mediterranean cabbage, genome C<sub>o</sub>C<sub>o</sub>) and <em>B. rapa</em> (Asian cabbage or turnip, genome A<sub>r</sub>A<sub>r</sub>) and is polyphyletic (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>), with spontaneous formation regarded by Darwin as an example of unconscious selection (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>). Cultivation began in Europe during the Middle Ages and spread worldwide. Diversifying selection gave rise to oilseed rape (canola), rutabaga, fodder rape, and kale morphotypes grown for oil, fodder, and food (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6"><em>6</em></a>).</p><p id="p-9">The homozygous <em>B. napus</em> genome of European winter oilseed cultivar ‘Darmor-<em>bzh</em>’ was assembled with long-read [&gt;700 base pairs (bp)] 454 GS-FLX+ Titanium (Roche, Basel, Switzerland) and Sanger sequence (tables S1 to S5 and figs. S1 to S3) (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). Correction and gap filling used 79 Gb of Illumina (San Diego, CA) HiSeq sequence. A final assembly of 849.7 Mb was obtained with SOAP (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8"><em>8</em></a>) and Newbler (Roche), with 89% nongapped sequence (tables S2 and S3). Unique mapping of ~5× nonassembled 454 sequences from <em>B. rapa</em> (‘Chiifu’) or <em>B. oleracea</em> (‘TO1000’) assigned most of the 20,702 <em>B. napus</em> scaffolds to either the A<sub>n</sub> (8294) or the C<sub>n</sub> (9984) subgenomes (tables S4 and S5 and fig. S3). The assembly covers ~79% of the 1130-Mb genome and includes 95.6% of <em>Brassica</em> expressed sequence tags (ESTs) (<a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). A single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) map (tables S6 to S9 and figs. S4 to S8) genetically anchored 712.3 Mb (84%) of the genome assembly, yielding pseudomolecules for the 19 chromosomes (table S10).</p><p id="p-10">The assembled C<sub>n</sub> subgenome (525.8 Mb) is larger than the A<sub>n</sub> subgenome (314.2 Mb), consistent with the relative sizes of the assembled C<sub>o</sub> genome of <em>B. oleracea</em> (540 Mb, 85% of the ~630-Mb genome) and the A<sub>r</sub> genome of <em>B. rapa</em> (312 Mb, 59% of the ~530-Mb genome) (<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>). The <em>B. napus</em> assembly contains 34.8% transposable elements (TEs), less than the 40% estimated from raw reads (tables S11 to S14) (<a id="xref-ref-7-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>), with asymmetric distribution in the A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes (table S12) as in the progenitor genomes (<a id="xref-ref-9-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-11-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>). A small TE fraction has proliferated since <em>B. napus</em> separated from its progenitors (<a id="xref-ref-7-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>), at lower rates in the <em>B. napus</em> subgenomes than the corresponding progenitor genomes (table S14 and figs. S9 and S10).</p><p id="p-11">The <em>B. napus</em> genome contains 101,040 gene models estimated from 35.5 Gb of RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data (tables S15 and S16) in combination with ab initio gene prediction, protein and EST alignments, and transposon masking (<a id="xref-ref-7-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). Of these, 91,167 were confirmed by matches with <em>B. rapa</em> and/or <em>B. oleracea</em> predicted proteomes. Genes are abundant in distal euchromatin but sparse near centromeric heterochromatin (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>). RNA-seq data revealed alternative splicing in 48% of genes, with frequent intron retention (62%) and rare exon skipping (3%) (tables S17 and S18 and fig. S11).</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F2">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="The genome of the B. napus oilseed cultivar &#x2018;Darmor-bzh&#x2019;.The genome comprises 9 chromosomes belonging to the Cn subgenome and 10 to the An subgenome, scaled on the basis of their assembled lengths. Tracks displayed are (A) gene density (nonoverlapping, window size = 100 kb for all tracks). Positions showing loss of one or more consecutive genes are displayed (triangles) along with homeologous exchanges, detected as missing genomic segments that have been replaced by duplicates of corresponding homeologous segments (red rectangles). (B and C) Transcription states estimated by RNA-seq in leaves (B) and roots (C) (in nonoverlapping 100-kb windows). (D) DNA transposon density. (E) Retrotransposon density. (F) CpG methylation in leaves (green) and roots (brown); both curves are overlapping. (G) Centromeric repeats (densities exaggerated for visual clarity). Homeologous relationships between An and Cn chromosomes are displayed with connecting lines colored according to the Cn chromosomes." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-455280786" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 2&lt;/span&gt;  &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;The genome of the &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt; oilseed cultivar &#x2018;Darmor-&lt;em&gt;bzh&lt;/em&gt;&#x2019;.&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-12&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;The genome comprises 9 chromosomes belonging to the C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; subgenome and 10 to the A&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; subgenome, scaled on the basis of their assembled lengths. Tracks displayed are (&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) gene density (nonoverlapping, window size = 100 kb for all tracks). Positions showing loss of one or more consecutive genes are displayed (triangles) along with homeologous exchanges, detected as missing genomic segments that have been replaced by duplicates of corresponding homeologous segments (red rectangles). (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;C&lt;/strong&gt;) Transcription states estimated by RNA-seq in leaves (B) and roots (C) (in nonoverlapping 100-kb windows). (&lt;strong&gt;D&lt;/strong&gt;) DNA transposon density. (&lt;strong&gt;E&lt;/strong&gt;) Retrotransposon density. (&lt;strong&gt;F&lt;/strong&gt;) CpG methylation in leaves (green) and roots (brown); both curves are overlapping. (&lt;strong&gt;G&lt;/strong&gt;) Centromeric repeats (densities exaggerated for visual clarity). Homeologous relationships between A&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; and C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; chromosomes are displayed with connecting lines colored according to the C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; chromosomes.&lt;/p&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F2.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F2.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 2"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/573168" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F2-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 2</span> 
<span class="caption-title">The genome of the <em>B. napus</em> oilseed cultivar ‘Darmor-<em>bzh</em>’.</span>
<p id="p-12" class="first-child">The genome comprises 9 chromosomes belonging to the C<sub>n</sub> subgenome and 10 to the A<sub>n</sub> subgenome, scaled on the basis of their assembled lengths. Tracks displayed are (<strong>A</strong>) gene density (nonoverlapping, window size = 100 kb for all tracks). Positions showing loss of one or more consecutive genes are displayed (triangles) along with homeologous exchanges, detected as missing genomic segments that have been replaced by duplicates of corresponding homeologous segments (red rectangles). (<strong>B</strong> and <strong>C</strong>) Transcription states estimated by RNA-seq in leaves (B) and roots (C) (in nonoverlapping 100-kb windows). (<strong>D</strong>) DNA transposon density. (<strong>E</strong>) Retrotransposon density. (<strong>F</strong>) CpG methylation in leaves (green) and roots (brown); both curves are overlapping. (<strong>G</strong>) Centromeric repeats (densities exaggerated for visual clarity). Homeologous relationships between A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> chromosomes are displayed with connecting lines colored according to the C<sub>n</sub> chromosomes.</p>
<div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-13">The <em>B. napus</em> A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes are largely colinear to the corresponding diploid A<sub>r</sub> and C<sub>o</sub> genomes, with asymmetric gene distribution (42,320 and 48,847, respectively) and 93% of the diploid gene space in orthologous blocks (fig. S12) (<a id="xref-ref-7-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). We identified 34,255 and 38,661 orthologous gene pairs between the A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes and their respective progenitor genomes (fig. S13). Comparison of A<sub>n</sub>-A<sub>r</sub> and C<sub>n</sub>-C<sub>o</sub> orthologous gene pairs suggested a divergence 7500 to 12,500 years ago (fig. S14), indicating formation of <em>B. napus</em> after this date. Synteny with <em>Arabidopsis</em> (table S19) confirmed the triplicated mesoploid structure (<a id="xref-ref-9-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-11-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>) of the A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes, with the recent allopolyploidy conferring on <em>B. napus</em> an aggregate 72× genome multiplication since the origin of angiosperms (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-7-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>).</p><p id="p-14">Most orthologous gene pairs in <em>B. rapa</em> and <em>B. oleracea</em> remain as homeologous pairs in <em>B. napus</em> (tables S19 to S25 and figs. S12 to S17) (<a id="xref-ref-7-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). DNA sequence analysis (<a id="xref-ref-7-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>) confirmed the loss of 112 A<sub>n</sub> and 91 C<sub>n</sub> genes in <em>B. napus</em> ‘Darmor-<em>bzh</em>’ (tables S21 to S26), ~2.6 times higher than the 41 and 37 genes lost in <em>B. rapa</em> ‘Chiifu’ and <em>B. oleracea</em> ‘TO1000’ respectively (tables S26 and S27; χ<sup>2</sup> test <em>P</em> = 5.3 × 10<sup>–14</sup>). Further analyses of a <em>Brassica</em> diversity set showed that ~47% of Darmor-<em>bzh</em> A<sub>n</sub> and 31% of C<sub>n</sub> deleted genes were also deleted in at least one additional progenitor genotype (tables S28 and S29), indicating that their deletion probably predated allopolyploidization of <em>B. napus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). A high proportion (27% to 54%) of the remaining Darmor-<em>bzh</em> deleted genes were also deleted from diverse <em>B. napus</em> genotypes (tables S28 and S29).</p><p id="p-15">Homeologous exchanges (HEs), including crossovers and noncrossovers, are frequent between <em>B. napus</em> subgenomes and range in size from large segments to single SNPs (<a id="xref-ref-7-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>) (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>, figs. S17 to S24, and tables S30 to S39).</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F3">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="HEs between B. napus chromosomes An2 and Cn2.(A) Coverage depth obtained along the An2 chromosome after mapping Illumina sequence reads from seven natural and one resynthesized B. napus genotypes (named on the right) to the reference genome of B. napus &#x2018;Darmor-bzh.&#x2019; (B and C) Coverage depth obtained for Ar2 and Co2 chromosomes, respectively, after mapping &amp;gt;21 genome-equivalents of Illumina sequence reads from B. napus Darmor-bzh on the B. rapa and B. oleracea genome assemblies concatenated together. (D) Similar to (A), where the Cn2 chromosome of Darmor-bzh is displayed. Segmental HEs are revealed based on sequence read coverage analysis, where a duplication (red) is revealed by significantly greater coverage for a given segment than the rest of the genome (black) and a deletion (blue) by little or no coverage for the corresponding homeologous segment. Sizes of chromosomes are indicated in Mb. An-to-Cn converted genes (at 60% or more conversion sites) are plotted as blue dots on Ar2 (B) and red dots on Co2 (C). Cn-to-An converted genes are plotted as blue dots on Co2 (C) and red dots on Ar2 (B). Open circles denote entirely converted genes using the same color code. Light gray lines connecting (A), (B), (C), and (D) indicate orthology relationships, and dark gray lines highlight segmental HEs in Darmor-bzh (names and descriptions detailed in table S31). Further HEs occurring between other homeologous chromosomes are shown in fig. S19. Black arrows in (A) indicate HEs involving GSL and FLC genes." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-455280786" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 3&lt;/span&gt;  &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;HEs between &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt; chromosomes A&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt;2 and C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt;2.&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-16&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;(&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Coverage depth obtained along the A&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt;2 chromosome after mapping Illumina sequence reads from seven natural and one resynthesized &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt; genotypes (named on the right) to the reference genome of &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt; &#x2018;Darmor-&lt;em&gt;bzh.&lt;/em&gt;&#x2019; (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;C&lt;/strong&gt;) Coverage depth obtained for A&lt;sub&gt;r&lt;/sub&gt;2 and C&lt;sub&gt;o&lt;/sub&gt;2 chromosomes, respectively, after mapping &gt;21 genome-equivalents of Illumina sequence reads from &lt;em&gt;B. napus&lt;/em&gt; Darmor&lt;em&gt;-bzh&lt;/em&gt; on the &lt;em&gt;B. rapa&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;B. oleracea&lt;/em&gt; genome assemblies concatenated together. (&lt;strong&gt;D&lt;/strong&gt;) Similar to (A), where the C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt;2 chromosome of Darmor-&lt;em&gt;bzh&lt;/em&gt; is displayed. Segmental HEs are revealed based on sequence read coverage analysis, where a duplication (red) is revealed by significantly greater coverage for a given segment than the rest of the genome (black) and a deletion (blue) by little or no coverage for the corresponding homeologous segment. Sizes of chromosomes are indicated in Mb. A&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt;-to-C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; converted genes (at 60% or more conversion sites) are plotted as blue dots on A&lt;sub&gt;r&lt;/sub&gt;2 (B) and red dots on C&lt;sub&gt;o&lt;/sub&gt;2 (C). C&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt;-to-A&lt;sub&gt;n&lt;/sub&gt; converted genes are plotted as blue dots on C&lt;sub&gt;o&lt;/sub&gt;2 (C) and red dots on A&lt;sub&gt;r&lt;/sub&gt;2 (B). Open circles denote entirely converted genes using the same color code. Light gray lines connecting (A), (B), (C), and (D) indicate orthology relationships, and dark gray lines highlight segmental HEs in Darmor-&lt;em&gt;bzh&lt;/em&gt; (names and descriptions detailed in table S31). Further HEs occurring between other homeologous chromosomes are shown in fig. S19. Black arrows in (A) indicate HEs involving &lt;em&gt;GSL&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;FLC&lt;/em&gt; genes.&lt;/p&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F3.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F3.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 3"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/345/6199/950/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/573640" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F3-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 3</span> 
<span class="caption-title">HEs between <em>B. napus</em> chromosomes A<sub>n</sub>2 and C<sub>n</sub>2.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">(<strong>A</strong>) Coverage depth obtained along the A<sub>n</sub>2 chromosome after mapping Illumina sequence reads from seven natural and one resynthesized <em>B. napus</em> genotypes (named on the right) to the reference genome of <em>B. napus</em> ‘Darmor-<em>bzh.</em>’ (<strong>B</strong> and <strong>C</strong>) Coverage depth obtained for A<sub>r</sub>2 and C<sub>o</sub>2 chromosomes, respectively, after mapping &gt;21 genome-equivalents of Illumina sequence reads from <em>B. napus</em> Darmor<em>-bzh</em> on the <em>B. rapa</em> and <em>B. oleracea</em> genome assemblies concatenated together. (<strong>D</strong>) Similar to (A), where the C<sub>n</sub>2 chromosome of Darmor-<em>bzh</em> is displayed. Segmental HEs are revealed based on sequence read coverage analysis, where a duplication (red) is revealed by significantly greater coverage for a given segment than the rest of the genome (black) and a deletion (blue) by little or no coverage for the corresponding homeologous segment. Sizes of chromosomes are indicated in Mb. A<sub>n</sub>-to-C<sub>n</sub> converted genes (at 60% or more conversion sites) are plotted as blue dots on A<sub>r</sub>2 (B) and red dots on C<sub>o</sub>2 (C). C<sub>n</sub>-to-A<sub>n</sub> converted genes are plotted as blue dots on C<sub>o</sub>2 (C) and red dots on A<sub>r</sub>2 (B). Open circles denote entirely converted genes using the same color code. Light gray lines connecting (A), (B), (C), and (D) indicate orthology relationships, and dark gray lines highlight segmental HEs in Darmor-<em>bzh</em> (names and descriptions detailed in table S31). Further HEs occurring between other homeologous chromosomes are shown in fig. S19. Black arrows in (A) indicate HEs involving <em>GSL</em> and <em>FLC</em> genes.</p>
<div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-17">At the chromosome segment level, HEs are characterized by replacement of a chromosomal region with a duplicated copy from the corresponding homeologous subgenome (<a id="xref-ref-7-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). We identified 17 HEs, 14 C<sub>n</sub> to A<sub>n</sub> and 3 A<sub>n</sub> to C<sub>n</sub> (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>, fig. S19, and tables S30 and S31). Sequences from seven diverse <em>B. napus</em> genotypes revealed both shared and specific segmental HEs. These are of varying sizes and are most frequent between chromosomes A<sub>n</sub>1-C<sub>n</sub>1, A<sub>n</sub>2-C<sub>n</sub>2, and A<sub>n</sub>9-C<sub>n</sub>9 (table S32, <a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>, and fig. S19). Larger HEs found in the synthetic <em>B. napus</em> H165 affect, for example, most of chromosomes A<sub>n</sub>1-C<sub>n</sub>1 and A<sub>n</sub>2-C<sub>n</sub>2 (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a> and fig. S19). Functional annotation of genes within HEs suggests some have experienced selection, contributing to the diversification of winter, spring, and Asian types of oilseed rape, rutabaga, and kale vegetables (<a id="xref-fig-3-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3B</a>, fig. S19, and table S33).</p><p id="p-18">We also identified 37 C<sub>n</sub> to A<sub>n</sub> and 56 A<sub>n</sub> to C<sub>n</sub> whole-gene conversions (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>) (table S34).</p><p id="p-19">At the single-nucleotide scale, exchanges between homeologous subgenomes account for ~86% of allelic differences between <em>B. napus</em> and its progenitors, with nearly ~1.3 times more conversions from the A<sub>n</sub> to the C<sub>n</sub> subgenome than the reverse (χ<sup>2</sup>, <em>P</em> &lt; 1.6 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) (tables S35 and S36). A total of 16,938 A<sub>n</sub> and 13,429 C<sub>n</sub> genes (with 10,258 from homeologous pairs) had at least two conversion sites (table S37); 842 A<sub>n</sub> and 579 C<sub>n</sub> genes were highly converted with 60 to 90% conversion sites (table S37).</p><p id="p-20">Transcript abundance indicated that 96% of genes are expressed in leaves, roots, or both (<a id="xref-ref-7-13" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>) (figs. S25 to S29 and tables S40 to S42). Subgenome and tissue effects and tissue-by-subgenome interactions were statistically significant (χ<sup>2</sup>, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.01), with 45 expression patterns (fig. S26) grouping into nine clusters (table S41).</p><p id="p-21">A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> homeologs contributed similarly to gene expression for 17,326 (58.3%) gene pairs (χ<sup>2</sup>, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.01) (fig. S27 and table S41). Both tissues showed higher expression for 4665 (15.7%) A<sub>n</sub> homeologs and 5437 (17.3%) C<sub>n</sub> homeologs (fig. S28 and table S41). There were 1062 gene pairs (3.7%) with higher expression of the A<sub>n</sub> homeolog over the C<sub>n</sub> homeolog in leaves, whereas the reverse was true in roots (fig. S28 and table S41). Conversly, for 966 gene pairs (3.3%), A<sub>n</sub> homeologs had lower expression than C<sub>n</sub> homeologs in leaves, with the pattern inverted in roots (fig. S28 and table S41).</p><p id="p-22">Gene expression is generally inversely related to CpG, CHG, and CHH cytosine DNA methylation levels (p is phosphate, implying a C is directly followed by a G, and H is A, C, or T) (<a id="xref-ref-7-14" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). Methyl bisulfite sequencing in Darmor-<em>bzh</em> (figs. S30 to S32 and tables S43 to S45) showed 4 to 8% higher methylation in C<sub>n</sub> genes than in their homoelogous A<sub>n</sub> genes (table S44), possibly because of greater transposon density in the C<sub>n</sub> subgenome (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2F</a>). Of the ~3100 gene pairs with differential gene body and/or untranslated region methylation between A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> homeologs in both roots and leaves, 51% were equally expressed. Only ~34% showed higher expression for the less-methylated homeologs, and the remaining ~15% showed the opposite pattern (table S45).</p><p id="p-23">It is interesting that partitioning of homeolog gene expression is largely established in <em>B. napus</em> with patterns of both genome dominance and genome equivalence. The absence of significant bias toward either subgenome of the recent <em>B. napus</em> allopolyploid contrasts with many old and recent polyploids (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13"><em>13</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>) but concurs with other old polyploids (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>).</p><p id="p-24">Oilseed <em>B. napus</em> has undergone intensive breeding to optimize seed oil content and lipid composition, decrease nutritionally undesirable erucic acid and glucosinolates (GSLs), optimize flowering behavior, and improve pathogen resistance.</p><p id="p-25">The expansion of <em>B. napus</em> lipid biosynthesis genes exceeds that known in other oilseed plants, with 1097 and 1132 genes annotated in the A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes, respectively (<a id="xref-ref-7-15" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>) (tables S46 to S48). Most lipid biosynthesis genes identified in the progenitor genomes are conserved in <em>B. napus</em>. For 18 acyl lipid orthologs, 3 and 2 genes appeared to be deleted from A<sub>n</sub> and C<sub>n</sub> subgenomes, respectively. Another 13 have been converted by HEs, nine from A<sub>n</sub> to C<sub>n</sub> and four from C<sub>n</sub> to A<sub>n</sub> (tables S47 and S48) (<a id="xref-ref-7-16" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>).</p><p id="p-26">Genetic variation for reduced seed GSLs also appears to be under breeding-directed selection. GSLs are sulfur-rich secondary metabolites important for plant defense and human health (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19"><em>19</em></a>); however, high levels in seeds form toxic breakdown products in animal feeds (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>). All 22 GSL catabolism genes identified in <em>B. rapa</em> and <em>B. oleracea</em> (<a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10"><em>10</em></a>) are conserved in <em>B. napus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-17" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>), and orthologs of only three C<sub>o</sub> and one A<sub>r</sub> GSL biosynthesis genes are missing (table S49). One deleted homeologous pair, corresponding to orthologs of <em>B. oleracea</em> <em>Bo2g161590</em> and <em>B. rapa</em> <em>Bra02931</em> , colocates with two quantitative trait loci (QTLs) for total aliphatic GSL content (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>) and corresponds to a HE in which a segment of A<sub>n</sub>2, with a missing GSL gene, has replaced the C<sub>n</sub>2 homeolog (<a id="xref-fig-3-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>). Two additional QTLs for aliphatic GSL content (<a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>) colocalize with a deletion of the <em>B. rapa</em> <em>Bra035929</em> ortholog on A<sub>n</sub>9 and its nondeleted homeolog on C<sub>n</sub>9 (<em>BnaC09g05300D</em>, fig. S17).</p><p id="p-27">We identified 425 nucleotide binding site leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) sequences encoding resistance gene homologs (245 on C<sub>n</sub> and 180 on A<sub>n</sub>). Of these, 75% (153 A<sub>n</sub> and 224 C<sub>n</sub>) are syntenic to A<sub>r</sub> and C<sub>o</sub> progenitors (<a id="xref-ref-7-18" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>) (table S50 and figs. S33 and S34). We confirmed the absence of five NBS-LRR genes from the A<sub>n</sub> subgenome, three from the C<sub>n</sub> subgenome, and three from <em>B. rapa</em> (A<sub>r</sub>), with none absent from <em>B. oleracea</em> C<sub>o</sub>. This variation may reflect differential selection for resistance to diseases.</p><p id="p-28"><em>B. napus</em> morphotypes show broad adaptation to different climatic zones and latitudes. A key adaptive gene controlling vernalization and photoperiod responses, <em>FLOWERING LOCUS C</em> (<em>FLC</em>) is expanded from one copy in <em>A. thaliana</em> to four in <em>B. rapa</em> and <em>B. oleracea</em> and nine or more in <em>B. napus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-7-19" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>) (table S51). Different <em>FLC</em> homologs lie within HEs, from C<sub>n</sub>2 to A<sub>n</sub>2 in the Asian semiwinter oilseed forms Yudal and Aburamasari (<a id="xref-fig-3-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>) and C<sub>n</sub>9 to A<sub>n</sub>10 in late-flowering swedes (fig. S19 and table S51). These loci correspond to important QTLs for vernalization requirement and flowering time (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22"><em>22</em></a>).</p><p id="p-29">Human cultivation and breeding of <em>B. napus</em> morphotypes may have selected favorable HEs, causing subgenome restructuring of regions containing genes controlling valuable agronomic traits such as those shown here for oil biosynthesis, seed GSL content, disease resistance, and flowering. Because <em>B. napus</em> is a young allopolyploid beginning gene loss and genome reorganization, further partitioning of expression may become a key determinant for the long-term preservation of its duplicated genes (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23"><em>23</em></a>). The integrative genomic resources that we report provide unique perspectives on the early evolution of a domesticated polyploid and will facilitate the manipulation of useful variation, contributing to sustainable increases in oilseed crop production to meet growing demands for both edible and biofuel oils.</p><div class="section app" id="app-1">

cit-pub-id-pmid"><span class="cit-pub-id-scheme-pmid">pmid:</span>18227114</span></cite></div><div class="cit-extra"><a href="{openurl}?query=rft.jtitle%253DBioinformatics%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Adoi%252F10.1093%252Fbioinformatics%252Fbtn025%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Apmid%252F18227114%26rft.genre%253Darticle%26rft_val_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Ajournal%26ctx_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ctx_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Actx" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a><a             <p  class="highwire-cite-metadata byline"><em>Science</em><time>22 Aug 2014</time> : 950-953 </p>
                <div class="highwire-cite-snippet media__deck">
      <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="section precis" id="abstract-2" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><p id="p-4">The polyploid genome of oilseed rape exhibits evolution through homologous gene conversion.</p></div></div>    </div>
